RATING: 8.5
I enjoyed Endeavor, though I must say I will need to get it on the table a few more times. This was fun. The most interesting aspect of this game is that in the opening moves, you're constantly amazed at just how BIG it is -- you're convinced that you will never make any progress, as it appears that there simply aren't enough actions to do so.

Then, magically, you're finished -- and the game is all opened up. It's quite amazing how a game can sneak up on you when you're busy playing it. THAT is the fascinating thing for me. I will pay attention to see if this remains the case with subsequent plays.

RATING: 7
1 play / 5 players

LIKE: Simple turns and ruleset, and contains interesting engine-building strategy. I like the randomness of the starting chits and they offer plenty of combos to look for.

DISLIKE: The notion of slavery is a negative, but it's thematic and not insulting. Five players might be a little much because the shipping lanes fill quickly. The game is abstract, which may turn some people off.

OVERALL: I liked it, I want to play again. Reminds me of another game, The Golden Ages. There were a few tense moments, but as I fell behind I wasn't sure what to do. Shipping seems important enough to own at minimum two tiles on your board.

RATING: 8.5
Endeavor is an excellent game. I would describe it as mechanically elegant. It sets up easily, is easy to teach, plays very quickly but involves a great deal of thought and strategy when playing. You play one of several European powers expanding their influence at first in Europe and then abroad. Your goals shift through the game from colonizing to shipping and unlocking new regions to exploit and then colonizing again. You attempt to become regional governor of new territories, to conquer more land, to collect victory points through building trade routes, and collecting specific sets of cards. You are able to customize the sorts of actions your empire takes as the game progresses by building a personal tableu of actions in the form of buildings and using your labor pool to power them. Will you be a militarty empire bent on conquest or a shipping empire based on expansion and colonization? A little of both? Will you build your empire on the backs of slaves or be known as the nation that abolished slavery? The game is just good. Thematically, mechanically, and artistically it is a master piece and it is a shame that it is out of print. Well, except I kind of like having one of the only copies around in my group :p Some people may say that, like in Five Tribes, this game is offensive because it is "about slavery". Not so. This game is about empire building and it does not white wash that slavery was a part of Europe and the worlds history when it came to building these empires. However, it also does not look away from the fact that slavery was eventually abolished. Endeavor introduces an ingenious mechanic that treats slavery sort of like the empire equivalent of steroids. It is good for some quick short term gains but if it is abolished, the empire takes a hit to VP as the world evolves a conscience in regards to the practice. In my opinion, the game includes slavery thematically and mechanically and this does not make it a bad game it makes it an honest one.

RATING: 8
Feels like a civilization game that does not take very long at all. There is war, trading, exploration and shows the slave trade in a realistic light (It was profitable at the same time as cruel). The lucrative trade lanes and links are also very thematic as one tries to gain them or pry them away through war or otherwise. There can even be negotiations with the right crowd. 
Downside is the fact that this is a game that scales better with more players to its max and needs minimum 3 to play. 
I really enjoyed the game and is highly recommended.

RATING: 7
A very streamlined worker placement game with tons of visual cues and icons to figure out what's going on. However, it is very thin on theme to the point of abstraction and is very much a token pusher. Another minor downside is that you don't know who has what points until game end, so you're never sure who the leader is to "attack". 

We definitely like Endeavor better than Puerto Rico or Caylus, but we remember liking Cuba better. Still, I can see Endeavor as being a solid game if you want to play a streamlined worker placement game within 90 minutes with 5 players. Our game is missing one red cube.

RATING: 7.1
It's a good game, and, if you look very, very closely, you just might be able to see the theme. If the theme were more tied in to the mechanics, I might rate it higher. Actually, with more plays, I might rate it higher regardless, but I can't imagine my rating reaching an 8. Some people complain about a game if there's nothing new in it, but that doesn't bother me if the familiar elements are tied together in a challenging new way, or even if the mechanics are simply rethemed. However, Endeavor feels like a number of games that I have played with nothing new about it, and an almost absent theme. If I played this five years ago, I'd be a lot more excited about it, but as it is, I can only say it's a nice game. Oddly enough, given the number of people who have said it's Struggle of Empires lite, I now very much want to try Struggle of Empires! Apart from that description, though, I would not call Endeavor light anything, apart from theme. And the board is quite unattractive.
Endeavour can be cutthroat, if you want it to be, and that doesn't bother me. But it does bother me that the person you're stabbing doesn't get any benefit from it, as you would in games like Carson City, Hansa Teutonica, or even Rheinlander.
A tad on the overhyped side.

RATING: 7
3-5(4,5)

2p variant. I like it quite a bit. It's actually quite short (almost too short, only two regions unlocked). I have yet to explore the balance (is colonization / attacking too strong? etc.) but a lot of fun choices and the neutral player does get in the way. A LOT of recordkeeping with the neutral player, though (have to remember to place him). It is exactly the sort of game I enjoy. Dry, in a sense, but also very well put together. Does have the Agricola-like feeling of "the game's over? But I'm just getting started!" in that it's pretty much impossible to advance all your tracks, build a level 5 building, ship, colonize, etc. in a single game.

Takes a while to set up, though. Geez. Kind of sucks that you set up a billion little chits on the board and you only unlock two regions, meaning that like 40% or more of that set up was for naught (well, I guess you make decisions on where to ship and colonize based on the layout). Still.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Endeavor is a well crafted combination of familiar elements.  Everybody mentions the Puerto Rico-esque buildings and the Goa-style tracks, which allow you to do more the further you progress. It is all quite streamlined and easy to learn. At heart it is about building an engine to maximise your actions.

The element that you don't expect in this sort of pure bred euro is war; though it is in a very simple and abstracted form.  Indeed, despite the exploration and empire building gloss, the whole game feels fairly abstract.

Endeavor is a perfectly well designed game that did little for me.  It presented some opportunities to do clever things and yet failed to excite.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I normally don't like "no luck" games, but I do like this one. You don't have to look too far ahead to do well, the random chit layout makes each game somewhat different, attacking is simple with automatic victory and used sparingly, and your choices are sometimes difficult and always meaningful. The main downside I see is that the higher number cards provide so many VPs, that you almost have to ship to one or two regions and gain a presence of 4 or 5, so you can draw a couple of "4" and/or "5" cards at the end of the game for lots of late VPs. I suspect this tactic will evolve into every player's endgame plan. Also, the game lends itself to AP in the last round.

RATING: 6
Surprisingly simple core despite an initial veneer of complexity.  I disagree with those that file this as a tech tree game, since there are only four simple and parallel tracks to push with no unlocking of higher ones.  I think its accessibility promotes replay, but especially the feeling that it seems imminently capable of being optimized.  Fun for now, but I feel like it's going to take "settler-quarry" to a whole new level pretty quickly, and probably along unequal turn order lines.  Initial chit distribution affects a lot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Thoughts after three 2-player games (variant in the forums) and one 5-player game.

Games are very tense and competitive.  All the games that I have played of this have had a lot of player interaction and conflict: there has been a lot of competing for governor cards and connections.  Buildings go fast in the 5 player version (not an issue in 2P).  There is just enough attacking to keep you on your toes.  

The high level of DIRECT interaction is HUGE breath of fresh air when compared to so many other low-interaction or indirect interaction euros out today.  In a way, the  interaction in Endeavor is on par with that of older euro games that didn't shy away from direct player-vs-player conflict, games like Settlers, El Grande, and T&E.  This a big plus in my book.

The game is easy to teach, and it is not difficult to fare at least decently on your first play.  Getting good at the game looks like it will take some time, and my initial plays give me a taste of a lot of hidden depth.

RATING: 7
Mechanically, the two most prominent elements of this game are engine-building and area-control. The engine-building aspect allows you to increase build-level (can draft more powerful buildings), increase growth-level (can get more workers), increase salary-level (can free up used buildings), and increase political-level (can hold more cards, which often increase other levels). The area-control aspect gives players access to different markets (so they can purchase cards) and feels like a race with the cards (limited spots, only can get/buy card if you have x spots occupied). Furthermore, the area-control aspect allows players to attack each other, but it is costly to the attacker and often negligible to the defender. In other words, it is well-controlled and doesn't open itself to meta-gaming, like other area-control games.

While this fits comfortably in the euro-genre stereotype, this is far from a solitaire game. The depth and richness of the game comes out with the synergy between the players. Understanding what your opponents are attempting to do and positioning yourself to benefit is really what the game is about. And despite a very streamlined and medium-weight ruleset, you always seems to have a plethora of options.

Overall, this game strikes a wonderful balance between tactics and strategy, manages to play quick with a high number of players, and has mechanics that facilitate interesting decisions while remaining easy-to-understand. Simply put, this is a very good game. If I had to complain, I would say the game is a bit fiddly to set-up and it really works best with larger groups (4-5 players). Other than that, it is a wonderful game.

RATING: N/A
I was interested in this game due to its quick play.  Thanks to a fellow BGGer, I have been spared buying the game that I would later sell anyways.  Below is the observation that was the deal breaker for me.

[q="Sorp222"]I have read some where that it is hard to make a really bad decision in the game. I would think that a weakness in the game design but I don’t think that is true in Endeavor. I have noticed that sub optimal play hands a lot of good stuff to the player on your left . My first game the player on my left  won  partly because I played badly. After the early discovery games I think it will be best enjoyed played with gamers of a similar standard.

...

[b]‘Puerto Rico’ factor[/b]

Because the scores are so tight in a game less experienced players can, inadvertently, hand victory to some one else. I have benefited more than I have suffered from this, but it makes me think that it is especially the case with Endeavor that the game will be enjoyed by playing with players of a similar experience. [/q]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First Play: 5 Player game. 

After rules were explained, strategies weren't apparent immediately (to me at least) and I made a couple of sub optimal moves that doomed me for the rest of the game. A solid  foundation in the central board is essential to get cards which give you a leg up in revving up your production engine. I suffered early loses here and I was never able to gain a slavery card (or indeed any central cards) and as a result brought up the rear for the rest of the game.

I imagine the game will vary considerably dependant upon the number of players with 5 being the most pressured and cut throat. 3 should be quite relaxed.

Starting position may also have a dramatic effect on strategy. Go first in a 5 player game (when there appears to be little or no advantage) and you will be picking up the least advantageous options for the next 4 turns when the game is coming close to a conclusion.

Certainly an opportunity for King making and bash the leader (which I don't mind) and an aggressive game where players can cripple an opponant early on if they are minded to do so.

However I did appreciate the depth and thought required for the game, but beware AP and I think a 3 minute timer is essential in order to keep the game flowing. Our first game with 3 beginners and 2 that had played before lasted 150 minutes.

I look forward to playing again.

Second Play (8 years later): Game was sprung as an option and I had forgotten 95% of it after such a long interval. Whilst giving the impression of multiple paths to victory, this isn't true. All new players must be told that shipping is crucial without it you will go no where. If you stumble in the early game you won't recover. Latter stages are very prone to AP and the last two turns seemed to be excessive exercises  in bean counting as players sought to maximise the efficiency of their turns. 

A dry experience that I see no need to repeat. I have much better games in my collection that need to be played. For sale.

RATING: 7
Want to play again. Area control game where euro-centric players lay claim to different parts of the new world to score points in the four areas of industry, culture, finances, poltics, and general imperial awesomeness. You keep track of your progress and capabilities on player boards similar to those used in Goa or kingsburg. The game attempts to deal with the historical contribution of slavery by representing it with a novel mechanic that penalizes overuse. I can appreciate that a game of this subject matter would at least acknowledge it rather than hide it away or pretend it was never a factor by not including it, but I'm not convinced that it adds that much to the game. I do want to play this again soon, so my bgg rating is a 7. I suspect, however, that it will probably settle down to a 6.

RATING: 6.5
An okay game. Feels somewhat scripted because some building tiles seem to be better than others... and it was tough to run with a shield (card limit increasing) strategy because cards are not necessarily easy to obtain from the continents... but it's hard to say after just one play... I probably missed something critical in terms of "combos" to make the strategy work.

** UPDATE ** The trick with this game seems to be obtaining a lot of the "icons" from buildings and/or tokens instead of trying to get them all through acquiring the good cards... because you have a limit on the number of cards you can hold.  Also, you don't need governor cards to win... if you can win one, great... but don't stress too much about winning one.  Governorships are hotly contested (i.e. cost a lot of discs/actions) and usually you can collect a comparable amount of good stuff from the rest of the board while other people are fighting over a particular governorships.

RATING: 5
Hmm, mechanically quite clever, and I like tech-trees, so that's a positive.

However it's a euro with a paper thin theme - I really don't get what the 'connection' bonuses are supposed to represent, trading network maybe? There's some attempt to flavour the colonies by the cards for each, but still ...

Don't mind the abstraction, in fact removing the bean counting for money is a big improvement and certainly speeds the game up, and it has a snowball effect, rich get richer, early turns to setup later ones and no 'catchup' mechanic, which I like. But doesn't help the theme though ...

Going later in the turn can be horrible, and depending on the random setup certain places are very obviously better.

Overall, the usual 'optimise your turn' type euro, quite easy to get into once you've played it once. Usual 'ahh' moments when you figure out play strategies, but in the end rather forgetable. Game didn't grab me with a 'must play again now, and this time I'll do X' as doing X is very dependant on game setup, seating order and what others do.

Inclusion of combat is interesting, but I'm not sure it fits, gives an element that you don't usually expect in a euro and I'm not sure it works ... can be quite costly in terms of efficency to start combat, and horrible to suffer an attack as you just lose vps and possible connections and card draw options. Not easy to remove it though, as it would make the combat buildings rather weak. Furthermore with some groups I could see people getting annoyed if you go down the combat route to get more interaction.

While I was playing I just kept thinking, I'd much rather be playing Age of Empires III. So, its a '5' for me - if you have it and want to play it, I'll play, but no desire to own a copy.

RATING: 8
Comprada la edición holandesa en pedido a bélgica en Julio 2012. Al final no me cogió el dinero Marc, y me lo regaló por mi cumple, junto con el brass. 

Juego de gestión de recursos  en los que cada jugador tiene que colonizar, abrir nuevas rutas de comercio, comerciar, etc.  El tablero está plagado de recursos y cada uno de ellos te permite mejorar factores del jugador que le permitiran hacer sus acciones con más efectividad.

El tablero está dividido en zonas. Hay recursos que se obtienen al conectar fortalezas adyacentes.. dentro de cada zona se aplica una mecánica de mayorías. 

En resumen. Es un juego muy completo de gestión de recursos. Si te gusta, por ejemplo, el Hansa, este juego te va a maravillar.

Vendido en Dic 2014 a Meridiano Editorial M6, a través de la bsk (concluimos el tema por gmail), por 28 euros, con gastos de envío a medias. Dió pena deshacerse de él.

RATING: 9
Unique, challenging development civilization game. An immediate top rating and purchase from a vendor at BGG.CON was earned upon first play, showing simplicity of play and interesting tactical choices. Beautifully done with theme, mechanics, and rules presentation combining for a perfect gaming experience. Medium depth with medium time of play (1 1/2 hrs) makes for a solid 9 rating, with every turn presenting fun, engaging game play as each player develops his shipping/colonization while trying to race for the points and block other players from scoring. The slavery cards are a clever thematic element, and at the same time offer difficult choices for getting ahead early at a future price. A little Puerto Rico (buildings providing special actions), a little Goa (developing your (4) areas), but a very different game from either of these classics.

RATING: 7
Endeavor was a gift from my Secret Santa.  This one came onto my radar once I read about the "official" 2 player variant.  The variant seemed clean and easy, and many many were singing its praises, so I put it on my wishlist.  And I must now join the choir, as the 2 player game is quite good.  Playing with more is tighter in the buildings area, but otherwise the change is not huge.  The game itself is pure pasted-on Euro, in the best possible way.  The random setup and the building sequencing seem like they'll keep the game fresh.  It is indeed Goa like (although I prefer Goa by far), but plays quite quickly.  Very easy to pick up and play - this is one solidly designed game in the tradition of good solid Euros.  Besides Brass, this is my favorite 2 player variant - works great!

RATING: 7
A tight, tense, confrontational Euro that can be really unforgiving if you make an early mistake. The interconnectedness of the tech track reminds me a little of Goa. Since you only have seven turns, you really have to plan several moves in advance, and some well-laid plans can be easily derailed by other players. Combat is a necessary and critical element of the game; you will lose badly if you do not engage in at least some combat some of the time. The slavery deck is a very interesting, if troubling, addition to the game, although I actually find it less offensive than similar (non-) treatments of the subject in other games (Puerto Rico, Archipelago).

As it is, I don't like how the game plays with three players: some sort of variant needs to be instated that limits the available regions of the board if you are playing with just three. Otherwise, it's a solid, good game.

RATING: 6
Updated 8/29/10.

Endeavor seems like it should be everything I want in a game: a semi-abstract Euro with a sailing/exploration theme, a small tech tree, and an official two-player variant.  For those same reasons, however, it is simply not the sort of thing that would stand out in my collection.  Nor does it stand up well against others in the genre.

I do (sort of) enjoy playing Endeavor when its suggested by others, but I find it too balanced to be worth owning.  It often feels like my decisions are irrelevant, and the game will end up with a close game no matter what I do.  I think much of that comes from two facts.  First, players will need to advance all skill tracks throughout the game.  Consequently, the game feels as though it lacks defined strategies.  No matter how I start, I always end having done a fair amount of everything.  There is no sense of specialization or differentiation between players, in my experience.  Second, all skill tracks advance at the same rate, score points at the same rate, and will be largely full by the end of the game.  At that point, an increase in any skill is as good as an increase in almost any other because they are all just worth one more victory point.  By making these tracks both so fungible and so abstracted, the designers have deprived the game of an interesting narrative arc.  I never feel like I'm crafting a strategy, but rather simply pressing the pre-designated buttons and watching the game run without me.  

This is, of course, is an oversimplification.  Poor play will easily lose you the game, and new players will probably see broader gaps between winning and losing scores.  But when played by experienced gamers, Endeavor seems to lack the strategy space to let the players take ownership of the game.  It is too concerned with accessibility and balance, and the safety net it attempts to provide becomes a straightjacket for heavier gamers.

In short, if I were at someone's house and they pulled out Endeavor, I'd gladly play.  But I won't buy it, and I'm more eager to try something refreshing, even if not as reliable.

RATING: 7.5
Solid game which was quiet enjoyable. I played a relatively high risk strategy in this first game to see how it would pan out, trying to score points from buildings and choosing area on the board that was less attractive (but still scored me points). Alas all 4 other players were military aggressors and the strategy didn't pay off. 

The balanced strategy payed off for the winner, although the scores weren't as far apart as I expected they would be. 

This is the kind of game you need to play through once to fully understand the scoring mechanism and appreciate the impact of each action on the end game situation. The scoring really ramps up in the final turn as the more powerful point scoring cards come out. 

The game also probably needs a central spot to place all your workers (like the ship in Puerto Rico) as we had some situations where players were (most likely) using the wrong workers (which potentially changed the end outcome). 

Solid - would play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
When Endeavor was first announced for Spiel 2008, I wasn't too thrilled about it. It was touted as a growth/snowball game, and I generally don't like those very much. But when the game was finalised and made its appearance in Europe during Spiel 2009, I had already turned around considerably. The reason: the well-written rulebook which gave me the impression that the game would at least be very clear and accessible, a must if I am to like such a game with growth strategies. With but an hour to spare for the closing of the day, I and four other people were rushed through the game, and I liked it sufficiently to take it home with me.

At the time of writing I've played a few more times, and can report that the feeling of playing a well-crafted game persisted. In fact, I probably like the game even more now that I'm beginning to understand how it 'ticks'. In most snowball games everything is locked up in the main positive feedback loop. You start out with money; money begets cards, personages, and buildings; these give you more money; then other components give you points; the end. But not so in Endeavor, where some of the corresponding items of the above scenario have been more or less taken out of the loop and are just 'given' to the player based on a very simple development setting. In this way, noone can truly be left behind (a very sore point with me in these games), allowing the players to focus on the fun part of the game: applying a strategy and attempting to see it through. The entire process reminds me, oddly enough, of Sylla, where a similar approach to model 'growth' has been taken. It all seems to work quite well and very smoothly, contributing to the game's feel good-factor.

Also helping the appreciation is the game's lavish production with large player mats and bright, clear iconography. My girlfriend likes the game too, although she isn't convinced that the game works at lower player numbers. The board is too big, she then complains. But I think that is precisely the beauty of this game: players use what they need so there is no actual need to open up more areas. In other words, Endeavor appears to scale very well, too. All in all: a more than pleasant surprise.

Follow on games. Three, four and five player affairs. 45 minutes (not including set-up). Awesome game. There is so much depth and strategy packed into simple mechanics and fast gameplay. Players can take almost any route to try for a victory and the games so far have been very close and exciting affairs. A must-try for any serious board gamer.

This is going to become (if it hasn't already) one of my favourite non-wargames, hence the 8 rating.

RATING: 5.5
Initial rating of 5.5 after one game, in which I tied for the win.  Interesting ideas which I like, but not a lot of excitement.  I'm not sure about the scoring cards.  You can (but I won't) spend agonizing minutes deciding which card you need to discard, carefully weighing the pros and cons of what you're losing in order to gain somewhere else.  

Besides the scoring cards there are multiple paths to analysis paralysis.  Buildings?  Actions?  Place here to win that chip or there to win this chip?  Or ship there to maybe have a chance to get that card so I can slow the revolutions of the earth down while I worry about what card I want to keep?  If I had a point for every time someone said "Whose turn is it?" I would have a lot of points.  

Would have more replay value if lasted an hour less.  But at least it's not an "engine building" game.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Endeavor is a combination of several mechanics from some of the best Euro games.  It has elements that are similar to Phoenicia (the game based upon The Scepter of Zavandor (which is based upon OutPost)), Goa, Puerto Rico, and the game ends after 7 rounds just like Princes of Florence.  So while the mechanics will not seem fresh to many people versed in the Euro style game, the combination of these elements work extremely well together.

Phoenicia:
In Phoenicia, you must manage your growth of Income, Workers, Hand size, and Victory Points.  In Endeavor, you must also manage your Construction Ability.  In Phoenicia, you can get upgrades along various knowledge tracks, but they are not laid out in a linear fashion the way they are with Goa.

Goa:
In Goa, your abilities explicitly move along 5 different tracks of knowledge.  Everyone has the same 5 knowledge tracks and a the total Victory Points at the end of the game is strongly determined by how far a player has moved along those tracks.  In Endeavor, you have 4 different tracks of knowledge (although your ability in each track may go down sometimes in exactly the same way as they might in Phoenicia).

Puerto Rico:
In Puerto Rico, you must not only build buildings, but you have to get workers to man the buildings.  This is almost exactly how Endeavor works, except Endeavor uses the removal of a worker from a building in lieu of getting gold (a very abstract representation that might bother some people). 

Board Position:
Unlike these previous games, there is board positioning in Endeavor.  This takes place mostly in the form of area majority conflicts, but also in mini skirmished over a city and it's trade route (a victory point + knowledge).

Overall:
Endeavor is a very well designed game that plays smoothly.  Since all of the information is completely open to everyone, it might get bogged down by people prone to AP (Clearclaw, I'm talking to you).  In fact, the last turn would suck if someone tried to figure out everyone's V.P. before taking their turns.

----  Edit ----
moved from 7 to 7.5.

RATING: 7
Players represent a European colonial power, exploring and expanding across the globe.  Players on their turn build a new building (giving stuff or allowing stuff, or both), get some population, free up buildings, then take turns to take actions.  Actions are ship across the globe, settle in a city, draw juicy cards, attack other players, and so on.  It's all about collecting glory, gained via advancing up your four area technology tree, taking cities, and so on.  Very clean game, inspired by many others, but taking the best bits and distilling it into a 30-90 minute game (depending on # of players and if you have thinkers present).  Was expecting a derivative knock-off, but pleasantly surprised.  Excellent game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I like the tech-tracks and buildings and board play and random set-up of tokens; the takeover mechanism is annoying, but somewhat expensive, so it didn't bother me too much.  What I really didn't like, though, is the chicken aspect to the shipping tracks and the cards.  Because of the tie-breaker, it's better to play later on the shipping tracks, and it's better to take later cards in a pile, so in both cases you want to wait until someone else goes.  This also means you have to pay attention to how you're setting up the next player at least as much as how you're benefiting from your move.  There's no randomness beyond the setup, so in theory you can evaluate the state of the world and predict what people will do, but at least on a first play I found it really hard to keep up with what everyone else was doing when I was still struggling with my own plans.  I'd like to play it again to see if it gets easier and if the chicken aspect bothers me less.  I also wonder if it would work to allow players to take any card from a stack (if they have enough presence) rather than only the top one; allowing players to place anywhere along the shipping track would be similar, but I'm not sure how to handle ties in that case.

RATING: 6
Good.  A nice evolution of the typical motif of building up your engine and rolling it into further development.  Gains points by filing off a lot of choke points (e.g. you can always build a building) and having a map to add asymmetries and a sense of place.  As a result the game feels like a Euro but a lot more open; you can actually go FOR things rather than constantly fighting just to be able to not starve, not go broke, etc.

The possibility of attacking is a nice addition as well; it isn't the whole focus but allows for a little direct competition.  One knock is the inability to disrupt shipping -- seeing how important privateers were during the game's setting, it would've been a nice option.

One concern is that the game has been so refined that it will play itself out quickly.  Time will tell.

Amusement point:  Some people, who have no problem with playing a game about dominating native lands and occupying their cities, do not like actual Slavery cards being present.  Apparently they only like games about slavery that don't explicitly mention it.

RATING: 6
Endeavor is certainly an engaging game that obviously has multiple paths to victory, but for reasons that partly elude me it is just not clicking with me, and my rating continues to slowly go down with time.

So far, I have only played with the maximum number of five players.  I think part of what I don't like about Endeavor is the chaos that comes with this number.  It is very difficult to make any long term plans and becomes more an exercise in reacting to the ever changing board and supply of buildings and cards.  There is also a real king-making phenomena where taking certain actions that you want or need can hugely benefit the players following you in that turn.

I will for now continue to play, but this game certainly is not one I would be interested in buying.

RATING: 7
High-level colonisation Euro that I quite like for its potential to play quickly. It's begun to cool on me, perhaps due to less variety in how games turn out, perhaps because of its kingmaking and leader-bashing capabilities (giving governor to others and attacks).

Duration: Actions are sequential but fairly small; the game can be played fairly quickly with the right people.
Design: Abstracted Industry, Culture, Finance and Politics tracks coupled with free buildings are the cleanest side of the design; buildings and colonists point towards Puerto Rico and are a bit fiddly. Card decks and buildings are always the same; randomness is only where the tokens are placed.
Strategy: There are actually a few ways to approach the game, and the continents give different styles of bonuses.
Components: I like the board and the clean play mats; the cards have little continent-themed icons but that's about it.
Theme: I haven't been into colonial-style games - Puerto Rico is fine mechanically but leaves me cold for theme - but this one caught my eye.

Note there is [i]no[/i] relationship with Cook's HMB Endeavour, and I wish they spelt it the UK way, and kept the Pacific map.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Feels very standard Euro to me, but I love Euros and this one brings a lot to the table.  One thing I don't like is it encourages you to be too balanced because if you go too heavy in one category, it will generally be a waste.  I can already see there's not going to be a huge gamespace to explore.  It's going to be about efficiency and tactics rather than strategy.

A lot of people really seem to like the interaction available in this game, but this is actually what ruins it for me.  Attacks are strong enough given the relatively small point spread from first to last (maybe 10pts) as to make your strategy over the course of the game relatively pointless.  Games can easily be won and lost through negotiation and diplomacy alone.  That's fine, but I think the game should focus more on that and less on the mechanics in that case.  What really annoys me about the diplomacy is that you really have nothing to negotiate with.  It falls into the "gang up on the leader or just generally whine your way to victory" category.  It's a zero sum game.  Besides colluding to hammer on the leader, there is nothing you can offer that is of relatively greater value to someone else.  If you want to see a game do diplomacy right, play Here I Stand.

RATING: 6
Although Endeavor is by and large a game about tech-tree development it doesn't have the strategic scale that these games usually come with (and actually need in order for that element to be meaningful). The four tech-trees are really just branches, and perfectly linear at that. They aren't even suited to differentiate strategies because you ultimately need to advance in all of them at roughly similar speed.

Progress on those four tracks is achieved by different means, and this is where most of the game is. It is, as mentioned above, much more about micro-managing your turns than a strategic affair. Since the tech-tree benefits always kick in at fixed points during your turn getting the right upgrade in at the right time might give you the crucial point for the win.

It is a highly polished and streamlined game that is sorely missing a few rough edges.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Compared to more recent games, the theme is not that strong here; but as an abstract game, it works.  The game is streamlined so that almost every decision that is made from the beginning of the game is noticeably impactful on one's own success.  For instance, forcing each player to build one and exactly one building every turn makes the game less thematic and less of a sandbox but one can see the relative performance differences immediately and throughout.

Despite any thematic shortcomings, there is still a sense of growth and exploration, and the art and visual design provide enough cues to give a richer sense of the actions one may take.

Sometimes, the game can become a game of chicken as people try to finish shipping routes.

A slight bit more closed information could add some spice and a non-point salad ending.

We will see how the Age of Sail edition changes things up!

RATING: 8
Die beste kürzeste Weltersegelung mit Sklavenverschleppung. MAGISTER NAVIS ist ein großes Kleinod, das die seltene Kunst beherrscht in kürzester Spielzeit eine große Spieltiefe zur Verfügung zu stellen. In nur rasanten sieben Runden, die verfliegen, setzt man Scheiben auf ein braunpastelliges Brett um dort thematisch neuen Kontinente zu erschliessen, Siedlungen zu gründen und über kolonialistische Ausbeutung seine vier Fortschrittsleisten zu fördern. Und mit Kanonen auf wehrlose Gegenspieler wird auch geschossen. Geschwindigkeit, Timing, Verbindungslinien und Vertreibungsaktionen sind dabei auf eine charaktervolle Art eloquent miteinander verknüpft. MAGISTER NAVIS gehört zu jenen selten Kreationen, die gut altern. Womöglich ist diese Nachbetrachtung, die eben ihre Zeit braucht, die wahrhaftige Beurteilungsgröße für ein sehr sehr gutes Spiel. In diesem Sinne ist MAGISTER NAVIS angekommen. Die integrierte Idee, die Abschaffung der Sklaverei als Tool zu implemntieren, das gegen Ende eine Partie zum Kippen bringen kann, ist brillant.

RATING: 8
A very interesting game with a lot of balance stuff going on that made it hugely intriguing.  A very straightforward Euro in some regards (acquire symbols to push up tracks to acquire other things, etc.) but I can't think of another game that does area control and set collection in quite the same way.  Lots of space for blocking and also supporting other players to benefit you (but not too much).  While only a small part of the game, the inclusion of slavery is intriguing as it gives quick bonuses but big penalties if slavery is later abolished.  In the end I do feel like I'm playing a game of symbols rather than feeling the breeze in my hair as a European explorer, but it all works so well together and I'm a fan of the Euro genre, so this really doesn't bother me.  The 3-player game feels a bit too open, but the 4-player game works quite well. It's unfortunate that the regions don't feel that distinct from one another, but overall this is quite an intriguing game that is wholly interesting when you play. Scores are often very tight!

After setting up the board, at first glance there looks to be a lot going on.  A few turns in and it all came together nicely. Still a little heavy AP the 1st time through. 
Being only 7 turns long it seems like a mistake early on could really hurt you.
I like how you need to mix the 4 tracks and how they work off of one another.  They seem well balanced too.    

Overall a solid medium to light Euro. Nothing really new here; but very well designed.

RATING: 7.5
Components: good quality
Complex: easy to teach, not hard to learn
Weight: medium
Players: 5, could work with 4
Length: around one hour and a half
Mechanics: area control, track developing...
Decisions: lot of
Opinion: really lovely game that offers what it promises, a tense game lasting around one hour and a half, with easy rules and really tense gameplay.
The game last 7 rounds with the same phases, build, take workers, pay workers, take actions adjust cards. So far so easy, but you have to look what others are doing, what are you aiming for and adapt yourself while following your strategy.
The developments are really nice, the tell you the level for your buildings, how many workers you will have this turn, how many you will pay from those working last turn and how many cards you can keep at the end of the turn.
Honestly, if the game would last more time, I would not enjoy it so much, but with this length the game is lovely.
Tension on the map (where to sail, where to colonize, who is next starting player, which actions others can do at any moment, which card to pick up....) and easy understanding of the rules.
If I would have to say a flow.... the starting player, it goes clockwise every turn, so the starting player will be so twice per game, while the last player on the last turn will only be once. But I have seen the last player winning games, so not much a flow.
We play with a small home rule, in the set up there cannot be blue tokens on Europe colonies, is a way to avoid the advantage of being first player at the beginning.

RATING: 5
Well-crafted but bland efficiency game of limited, uninteresting options. Try Hansa Teutonica for a better game with similar ideas.

Endeavor is a prime example of my least favorite tendency in Eurogames: games that are so finely balanced that all the interesting peaks and valleys are scraped away.  I'd much rather see a game offer items/actions of varying quality, and then provide a method for players to fight over them: auctions, majority, battle, depletion, manipulating order of play, sacrificing more immediate gains, or whatever.  If you're going to allow players to simply go around the table selecting a thingamabob, then those thingamabobs must be roughly equal in value, which is simply not exciting.  Good games need a little anxiety, and should not be prescribed Zoloft.

RATING: 7
While the game has the theme of nations expanding their empires across the globe, in reality the game is quite abstract.  The rules attempt to set the atmosphere by spinning tales of advancing one's civilization in the areas of culture, finance, industry and politics, while controlling vital trade routes between worldwide cities.  In reality, however, the flavor is mostly absent, as players are simply placing tokens and collecting cardboard chits, tracking their collection on their player boards.  The buildings players purchase simply enhance this collection process or allow a player to be more aggressive militarily.  Sadly, while the game paints an intriguing thematic picture, in reality the theme is little more than a ghostly presence.  That being said, the game itself is quite engaging and challenging.  There are numerous options and decisions to be made each turn, and a wary eye must be kept on one's opponents.  

All of my games have been tense, challenging and fun.  The game is quite fluid, giving players numerous options and strategies to pursue.  The games biggest drawback for me is its abstract feel, but fortunately the tense game play overcomes the lack of a tight theme.  While I don't think the game will set the gaming world afire, it still offers an engaging time for the contestants vying for the most advanced civilization.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
On initial play, I found this game to be fun and easy to learn, but with a fair amount of depth.  Cool that there is no hidden information, and no randomness except for the location of the starting chits, but the order of the players (and initial start player) is important.

There is a lot of balancing to do, such as between having enough people (urn track, and money track) and having buildings that have circles that can be filled by people.  Another thing to manage is getting consecutive colonies, at first, just to get extra chits, and in the end, to get connected networks for extra points.  (Watch out for people who have battle capabilities and lots of people -- they are looking for good colonies to fight for!)

Shipping is important; it might even be worth having 2 shipyards early.  There is a bit of a race, and a bit of playing chicken for shipping -- you want to have the most in a shipping track to get the governorship, and even if you don't, you might want to use the docks to finish the track so that you can get a colony as well.

With 5 players, the game is competitive but fun -- lots of battles for colonies, and cards often get drawn down to the end of the pile.  With 4 players, not all the regions get opened (because not enough shipping actions are taken), so it's important to leverage other players' help when trying to open a region (e.g. to win a governor card or colonize in a region in order to make a connection to a city you control in Europe).

RATING: 8
34/14= 2.43
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This seems to be an ultra light, ultra clean civ building game, along the lines of Phoenicia or Roll Through the Ages. Play is deceptively simple: 1) build a building based on the number of bricks production, 2) get colonists/workers based on the number of vases (imported goods), 3) get X workers back from buildings based on number of gold sacks, 4) take actions based on number of total workers, 5) check hand size based on number of victory flags.

Like other civ games, buildings give certain privileges and powers, but you can only build eight during the entire game. In fact, the game is only eight turns! This is similar to the tightness of A Castle for All Ages. Getting more workers is key, as this gives you more actions. Workers must man buildings, like in Puerto Rico, to activate them, but you need gold to remove them faster on subsequent turns. Shipping allows placement of workers in six colonial regions outside of Europe, thereby returning goods. More workers means more influence, and the most influence rewards players with a governor card, which gives more goods and VP. Whomever has the most VP, in terms of how much bricks/vases/gold/flags they have accumulated on their respective tracks, plus VP from occupying cities and trade routes, is the winner.

I admire the cleanness, purity, and simplicity of this design. Since I have only played it once, it is not enough to appreciate the nuances of the game mechanics, nor the various strategies that are available. Based on one play though, it seems too simple for my tastes, but I will have to try it several more times to see if there is any meat to this game, as I'm sure there is.

Update (08/17/10): You know what? I don't think I care to play this ever again. The civ feel is way too abstracted for the sake of shortening the game time, so there is no epic feel to it. Downgrading to a '6'.

Update (02/10/13): It's been several years, and I still have no desire to play this highly abstracted civ game. With Clash of Cultures out there, I think I'm done with this one. Downgrading to a '4'.

RATING: 6
This is a solid "building" game where your early decisions can have a large impact on how your endgame develops.  The game has a bit more of a tactical feel to it than Puerto Rico.  The buildings are not quite as unique and play focuses on try to gain many small advantages over time. Overall, I the game involves some good strategic moments.  However, I dislike how the game can easily devolve into stalling because doing something you really would like to see happen will help another player much more than you, and vice-versa.  Additionally, it feels a little bland... picking up little tokens that are randomly sprinkled on the board doesn't give you much of the feel of building an empire.

RATING: 6
A very abstract colonising game in which 'shipping', settling and conflict all feel equally bland. It reminded me of Agricola more than anything, a worker placement game with a lot of calculation in the ending. It's engaging enough, but there are no real competing strategies, the building track is the least important of the four and you win by creating the most city-to-city links. It's at its best with 4 players, with 3 not all areas open up, with 5 most spots are taken and options narrow considerably.
I've detected a recent trend to what I call 'gaming popcorn', light, fluffy and universally popular games of little real substance. This is the prime example, lets hope the genre doesn't continue.

RATING: 4
I was totally stoked to play this with 5. I was already mentally adding it to my next game order and wanted to seal the deal. Man, I'm glad I played it again!

5 player Endeavor just totally became a bloodbath. Now, I don't mind bloodbath games, but there were no checks or balances in our game. I attacked DJ a couple of times in key places and he just used his action to undo what I did. Meanwhile, Matt was totally placing himself in a great place for the win. I kept pointing out alternative locations to attack, but ultimately, DJ made the right decision to keep attacking me, because that would give him the most points.

The 4 player game was much more interesting, because while you could attack people, you could also build up your infrastructure and feel like you're building an empire. In the 5-player game, the whole last turn was comprised of attacking. There was no choice other than attack. All of the shipping was gone and the colonies were all taken, so that leaves taking cards (which you couldn't do, because everyone had already taken the cards that you were eligible for) or attacking. It just felt really chaotic and unexpected based on our 4-player game.

I won't be buying this one now and honestly, I probably wouldn't play this again with 5 players. I'd like to give it a go again with 4, but something tells me, now that we all understand it better, it will end up very similarly to the 5-player game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First played September 23rd, 2009. Taught in 15 minutes, played in about 90 minutes for 3 players. The game has a good strategy/luck mix. Lots of options to sift through. Each player begins with a player map that has one building on it. Victory points are award for advancing your tokens along the 4 tracks (building, round people, returning people, and card limit), capturing location on the main board and gathering other special VPs. There are 7 rounds to the game. You have a start player and it rotates each round. Each round players build a building based on their building ability.  Buildings either allow you to gain resources (move up the track) or take an action(s). Then each player is granted a certain number of people to do actions. People that did actions are also returned from the previous actions based on your track position. Next each player takes one action at a time and rotates until players don't have people or pass. Actions can be: settle, ship, take a card, bump someone off a settlement (extra person required). Each require a certain building to do the action. When you settle or ship you take the resource and upgrade the corresponding track. Same with cards. Each shipment island has six locations that need to be filled before the governor card is taken. This card may replace the final card slot on your personal player board, however you get 3 VPs for not replacing it. Great game that I recommend you try and consider purchase and placement in your rotation of game plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoyed this game quite a bit. Endeavor has some engine building, area control, a smidgen of 'take that', it is attractive looking game on the table. I would add a copy to my collection for the right price or trade. I like that the game doesn't take too long and that there is very little downtime between turns. I have only played once with three players. The game does not scale for the number of players and I imagine it being much more interesting with 4 or 5 players. Endeavor does not have a lot of variable setup: the connections between cities is always the same and the stacks of cards that you can get are always in the same sequence every game--yes, the tokens are distributed randomly all over the board each play, but the long run replayablity may be suspect. 8/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Endeavor is a really nice Euro.  Each player represents a colonial power.  Over each of the eight turns you get a free building that provides you with one or more actions.  You also get workers that you can place on the buildings to activate them.  The buildings may also provide symbols that advance you along several tracks that provide more workers or lets you pay them off freeing up both the worker and the building or that allows for better buildings or that increases your hand size of development cards.  Activating a building allows you to perform actions like occupying an empty city (initially just in Europe) or occupying an exploration space on leading to unlocking a new colonial region.  When you perform either of these actions you get a token that advances you along one of the tracks or provides a free action that you can perform later on.  The tokens are face-up so a lot of strategy involves figuring out how you can get the tokens you need the most.  If you take two connected cities you also get a token on the trade-route between them so there is a lot of maneuvering.  Once all the spaces leading to a colonial region have been occupied, the region is unlocked.  The player that played the most workers on the exploration spaces gets a valuable governor card for that colonial region that provides a number of advancements on the tracks.  The more workers you have placed in a region, the better the development card you can draw from it (which further advances you along your tracks) as another type of action.  So anyway, there is a lot of maneuvering that makes for a tense interesting game as you slowly building up both your personal position (on your tracks) and on the board.  You get most of your points from your position on your tracks and from the cities and trade-routes on the board.  There is lots of strategy and tactical maneuvering and different paths to victory (driven by the buildings you select).  I also like that it only takes a couple hours and with a clear game clock.  Very fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Update:

Game 16 saw a nasty case of kingmaking (putting me in last place and at the same time handing the game to another player already doing well). Left a sour taste... :-/
-------

Wow, it's been a long time since I've enjoyed a new game this much! Endeavor might not be terribly original or innovative game, but it certainly is an excellent piece of craftsmanship. All mechanics just work really well. Balance seems good, and the theme is even stronger than the abstractions used would seem to imply.

After a couple of games, I am very impressed and look forward to playing again.

Update: The game feels quite different depending on the number of players. The five-player game is tight; there is not enough space for everybody to just do their own thing, buildings get sold out, etc. At the other end of the spectrum, the three-player game feels much more loose and friendly.

I suspect the 'meanness' of the game will only increase with increased player experience, as players discover how to look at more than just optimizing their own progress.

RATING: 7
The highlights of Endeavor were the tech tracks and building powers.  It's been commented that the techs are reminiscent of Goa; the difference is that each tech is utilized each turn, and only once.

There is a mild area-control element in the cards and sea spaces, and some spatial mechanics in the connections.

There will be some maneuvering around slavery in most games, I suspect, with players either bring pro-slavery or pushing the card track ending in the abolition of slavery.  In games with more than three players I'd be surprised if slavery is abolished thanks to the crowding in Europe.  I wonder if some scaling with number of players is necessary with respect to available space and card requirements.  Slavery seems not powerful enough in 3p and probably too powerful in 5p.

**Yeah I'm a pretty big fan of this game.  The problem is that other's just don't care for it as much.  It does take a little while to set up...this is overblown by others who again will play it but would prefer to play Stone Age or Acquire.  

In terms of ideal path to victory...well I'd say no but maybe some moves are a bit scripted at times.  In terms of victory point count I'd say roughly...and I'll stress roughly 50 percent of your victory points are derived from tokens which are randomly layed out on the board.  There's numerous factors which come into play in terms of who ends up picking what token so you can't argue to much about the game being terribly scripted.  I guess the point I'm trying to make is that the game has enough randomness, strategy, replayability, and visual appeal to make it one of the top ten games for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The game is like Puerto Rico in that you collect buildings which govern your available actions, but that's where the resemblance stops. The game is much more like Goa, in which you advance along numerous tracks to increase the power of your actions. Endeavor introduces a few twists, some of which I like and some I'm pretty ambivalent about. I do like the idea of population and how it governs the number of actions you take each turn. I like that these actions can be saved from turn to turn and that they might block the usage of buildings if they don't get paid enough.

The geographic aspect of the board I'm not crazy about. I think it plays well, but it takes a long time to set up and I wonder how much different arrangements of tiles really affects the game. The game hints at being strategic, but I really think this is more of a tactical game. I think players have adequate control over the their actions so the medium weight doesn't bother me. I think what probably bothers me most is that I'd rather be playing Goa or Peurto Rico. The decisions in Endeavor just don't seem as sweetly difficult as those other games. Still this is solid and I know it will be enjoyed when I bring it out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

September 2010 Update:  Re-rated the game and dropped it to a 5.  It no longer has any appeal to me, think its rapid initial rise in the BGG rankings may not be justified.  I simply don't play it all that much any more, as other newer games are more interesting.

RATING: 6
Endeavor is nothing if not familiar. The theme and mechanics are largely cobbled together from Puerto Rico and Goa. Playtime is reduced. Gameplay is lighter but with a larger array of elements. In other words, it's derivative as hell but well done, so I can't knock it too much even if I'm not exactly blown away.

On the one hand, you've got the ever-present building paths that vary each player's abilities. They're simple, but still provide real choices. You've also got four capacities or techs to manage, and I don't even remember their names. Industry might have been in there somewhere. Maybe culture. The fact is, you could banish all real-world referents and learn this game just as easily. You've got four colored lines and you must increase your capacity on each one. Each one is a bottleneck that can be temporarily eased by advancing the others. Tech trees only work for me if each step does something unique, as opposed to easing progress somewhere else on the tree. The saving grace here is that you may fluidly adjust each level in a few different ways at different times in the turn. The opportunities for timing and sequencing are more refined than they are in many similar games.

On the board, there are various regions to colonize, but it's not a serviceable map in any way, just boxes labeled "Far East" or "Africa" with wavy lines connecting everything. A recent session report noted that, in retrospect, the players could clearly recall what part of the board they were invested in and why, but drew blanks regarding regions and cities. I'm not surprised. I contend that a themeless version of Endeavor would play just as easily, and before I'm taken to task for saying so, consider that that's actually a remarkable achievement for a game of this complexity. Undeniably, the moving parts here are well-oiled and easy to follow.

Endeavor is kind of a sad game from my point of view. The cards illustrate this better than any other aspect. You maneuver to pick up cards, and each one just has a handful of printed values to adjust your four colored lines or maybe your score. It's tough for me to get excited by replacing a card that has 1 green and 2 blue with one that has three yellow and three green. I will undoubtedly play this two or three more times, maybe more. A solid game, but too much was lost in the streamlining.

Update: Six months later, and I haven't tried it again. No desire to, really. There's nothing about the structure of this game that inspires any enthusiasm in me. It's a stripped down blend of games with solid mechanics and very little room for creative play. Nominal thematic integration.

--------------------------------------------
--------------------------------------------

After two plays one with 3 and one with 4.

This is a pretty good game. The rules are simple and there are a few cute mechanics. The most novel of which is having to pay your workers to free up buildings for future actions. You have to balance the number actions you have with the number of workers you get to perform the actions and the ability to free up those actions for the next round. 

There seems to be some variety in strategy, attacking/shipping/occupying. They are all somehow related; one must ship to open regions and the presence gained helps in getting cards. One must ship to be present so that they can occupy and all of this helps with cards. Attacking helps make connections increases your score for occupying and gives you more presence to get cards. So it would seem that the only constant is cards. But there exist very different types of card, ones that help with boosting your tracks and ones that mostly (or entirely) give VP. Deciding which of these cards will work for you and which building to take each round are some of the most critical decisions in the game.

I'm not yet sure if the replayability will be very high, it might get tiered and boring relatively fast, but it might become a lot more interesting as the subtle strategies reveal themselves...

RATING: 6
A slickly developed Euro, but is that a compliment or an insult?

To the good: the game is quite intuitive and flows smoothly, there are different paths you can pursue, and it's well balanced.

But: I'm not convinced the different paths are genuinely different, it may well be over-balanced.

To the bad: a feeling of lack of novelty, player interaction is mainly incidental rather than intentional, most decisions are very obvious.

A separate note to the good: Josh Cappel is an outstanding graphic designer, neatly balancing form and function.

RATING: 6
Why did they add the cannons? This element divides players. Conflict averse Euro gamers will take offence at the unrestricted direct player targetting. Fans of American game design will rejoice. Being a game of close scores while at the same time scores can be calculated only with high effort, Endeavor is prone to kingmaking based on imperfect information or simple gut feeling. Such a pity as otherwise it's a game of interesting tactical decisions with a relevant topographical component, something getting rare in contemporary Euros. Although it features "tech trees" which have to be advanced it's not a strategic game as you need any of these trees and usually take the one that is of more immediate short-term use.

RATING: 6
My opinion of Endeavor has been on a steady downward slide from
the first play on. After the first play I was eager for a rematch. It
played out pretty much exactly the same way as the original, and so
has every other game of it I've played. Even when people make what look
like bold opening gambits, by the late game the positions are just
the same kind of mush they were every other time.

Everything is awfully well balanced, to the point where it feels as if
what you do doesn't matter. Except when somebody really screws up, the
scores tend to be very tight. Every kind of action is about as
valuable, every player's position in the end of the game is going to
be very similar. Rather than winning due to superior strategic
planning, you win by getting beat up on less by the other players.

Basically Endeavor feels like a game where every sharp edge was filed
off during development, leaving behind a competent but bland experience.

[10/27/09: Yup, raising rating. It's a fantastic game. I've never won but have come close. Although, I recently played a game where one player decided the spaces I was occupying were prettier than anyone else's, and after he made repeated use of barracks, there was absolutely no way for me to recover. That player did not win, by the way, so it wasn't really the best strategy for him, either, but it wasn't goofy enough for me to lodge a sportsmanship complaint.

More games with finite resources and direct competition should take a page from Nexus Ops and give you something when another player starts hammering away at you and stealing your turf. It's plenty easy for someone to just decide you don't belong in this game, and hamstring you.]

RATING: 5
As far as "no random elements, no hidden information" -games go I guess Endeavor is ok, but unfortunately I prefer games to have both luck and hidden information. 

Since there is no randomness and each players score can be calculated with a bit of effort, Endeavor's last turns have *massive* downtime with serious players, since they can calculate each move and its effects on final scores.

For some reason Endeavor seems to be often compared to Struggle of Empires. Don't be fooled by this, they have similar theme but the games themselves have nothing in common.

RATING: 7.5
There is really something of both Hansa Teutonica (tracks dictating capabilities, map that you occupy) and El Capitán (islands to colonize, series and majority importance) here with a little Puerto Rico thrown in (buildings & workers).  Plays very quickly (always < 1h even with 4) which is very nice.  

Play itself has good depth but the breath of options & approaches can be overwhelming at first.  To this day, I still think the 1st turn of a given game is one of the most consequential and complex one.  There's also quite a learning curve to things.  Expect someone who already played to pretty much systematically beat someone who hasn't.  Once you get the hang of things though, it gets pretty fun & engaging.

For regular play, even with people who are not too hardcore, this is a pretty solid option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
the choices you're making are very tight, excellent well balanced game 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Not bad actually. Still I think the game fall a bit short in time. In our 5 player game, we should at least have another turn because there was 1 row of buildings which was never used and 1 continent did not had the chance to be colonized.

One of the problem I might see is that you have to plan a lot in advance to make sure you don't get stuck with unused people and buildings. I would have liked to have some compensation actions to use people you could not manage to use.

The random placement of tokens at the beginning of the game is excellent. Considering that there is no modular map at least you can have a bit of replay value.

The only other suggestion I could make would be that some buildings are pretty weak. The market never get's used and the Bank and theater does not give enough resources for an non-action building. Maybe 3 resources would have been better (instead of 2). But that is just balancing details.

RATING: 6
A better than usual Eurogame in my view.  It does offer some strategic decisions that are meaningful and have effect on one's later game development.  The game is of eurocentric view of the world where dominating Europe would almost guarantee a winning of the game.  How to factor in other side journeys over the world to one's overall plan of conquest in Europe is definitely required.  It seems to me it could achive its own objective of delivering a short but concise history of the Colonial period but then you might also find there could not be sligtest mistake made during the course of a game.  Eventually, it seems that there are many choices out there but in fact you could only coose a few of them.  Focus and stick to what you really want to do with your nation are central to your gameplay in a highly competitive environment.  The randomnization of the initial resources over the world might add replayability to the game - only if so you don't care if they are histroically rich in that particular region of the world or not.  Overall, a more "historical" Eurogame for anyone who wants to have a break from the more complicated wargame e.g. Pax Britannia or Viceroys.  After all, I won the game the first time I played it, even without reading the game rules.  So could anyone.

RATING: 8
At first this appears to be every other Eurogame out there: colonizing, shipping, little tokens, and so forth. Cracking it open and seeing the usual beige-on-tan color palette, I wasn’t expecting a lot. But it’s actually an interaction-rich, nicely thematic game that’s been stripped down to its essence, and plays surprisingly fast…almost too fast. This is the game you always suspect is lying underneath levels of auctions, cube transformations, action points, worker placement, and other junk. Endeavor pulls all that away and gets at the heart of the game, and it’s a fun, interesting game.

RATING: 7
The game focuses on Europe colonizing various parts of the world.  It is very abstract with a rough European map but no focus on countries or civilizations.  You build an economy by acquiring cards that provide you with resources.   You place tokens on the map to gain additional resources and benefits.  In some ways you have to work together to open up new areas to settle.  

Some players may be put off by the abstract nature of the game, but if you like majority control type games, this is very good one.

RATING: 7

I like it even better. It is among the most elegant and well-designed euros of all. With very simple tools, this game goes a long way.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[Played 4 times]

Resource collection and some drafting (buildings, cards). Minor direct interaction via wars (that can occasionally be annoying and lead to kingmaking), but mostly it is area majority.

Very fast first turns (when you have just an action or two), while later ones can be quite longer (and with more options, since areas of the boards get "unlocked" during the game). Overall, the length feels right and the decisions not trivial.

Deterministic gameplay, but random setup to offer greater replay value.

Theme is inexistent.

Nice game in its genre (optimization of actions, building an economic engine, scoring VPs to win), albeit it lacks a "soul" (i.e. some good reason to play it rather than the other hundreds of similar - and in some cases better - games).

RATING: 9
Very original game of which I know no equivalent. Thematically interesting. Plays pretty fast, even with 5 players, although a lot of difficult decisions have to be made. But your options are visually very clear.

One negative side is the importance of the actions of the player that is sitting just before you. If this is an unexperienced player, you have a considerable advantage.
Otherwise, a game that can be played by everyone. Easy to learn, easy to play, and all you do is always good for something, so running away in score is something I didn't see happening yet. 

Minus: 1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It's clever and fast, has a good dynamic arc and looks great, but my first game left me with the impression that a lot of offense moves against me took me out of the loop. Sadly, our second game confirmed that early attacks are moderately helpful to the attacker and can be absolutely deadly for the (more or less arbitrarily chosen) victim. Having it happen to someone else this time did not make it substantially better.

This mars an otherwise fine game to the point where I probably would not play again. I can't think of a promising cure, either, beyond the slight change that during setup, all attack action tokens placed in Europe are switched to somewhere harder to reach.

Played twice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]First impression of the game?[/b]
This game is in my sweet-spot. Some area control, limited turns, efficient action expenditure. It has some of the best elements of my favorite games (Princes of Florence, Goa, Puerto Rico).

[b]What are the highlights?[/b]
I think the overall game design is pretty clever. It runs smoothly and yet offers a lot of strategic depth.

[b]What are the drawbacks?[/b]
I can see where the A/P prone may go into "lock up" overanalyzing each outcome.

[b]Who would enjoy this game?[/b]
Eurogamers and light wargamers will dig this title.

[b]Who would [i]not[/i] enjoy this game?[/b]
I can already hear the Ameritrash crowd moaning about "too dry" and "too systematic."

[b]Would you play this game again?[/b]
I would very much like to play this again. I am giving it a provisional "9" because the luster may wear off after 10 or so plays. But I look forward to that possibility...

RATING: 7
Endeavor is a really nice, mechanically tight, game.  Its nothing we haven't seen before but its just put together so darned well.  I imagine it as the love child of Goa, Web of Power, and Puerto Rico.  In fact, if I had a spare hour, I'm not sure if I'd play Web of Power twice or Endeavor once.  Endeavor is probably the Goa-killer for me.

In my head, I could see at least two expansions for this game, one with new buildings, a new action type, and perhaps an added miniboard (perhaps papal influence or some such). The other would go the Imperial 2030 route and provide a second board in an earlier or later time frame, or perhaps the Axis & Allies: Pacific route and play out on a smaller scale in a specific location. This would also make a fantastic space colonization game.

RATING: 7
Only played once. I didn't get it, but I see something there. I'll need more plays (which I look forward to) to fully understand this one and better judge it. Until then I go with my first impression: 7/10 - better than most, not as good as others.
*UPDATE (Several more games played)* I want to like this as much as everyone else, but I just don't get it. I don't feel that I can create a clear strategy and feel that even if I could, it would have little effect. In addition, I feel that the game does not scale well with different number of players (2 vs 3 vs 4 vs 5), and a 2 player game is drastically different from a 5 player game. This means that you've got to play enough games with different number of players to know what strategy to implement and how much map you expect to get to play based on how many people are playing. Therefore, a new or even relatively new player will have a severe disadvantage to experienced players and will not have fun playing with them and will get thrashed in the end.

RATING: 7
11/28/09 Played a three-player game. This may go up if I'm able to play a four or five player game. Right now I don't rate it higher due to it not playing well with three. Plus, the graphics on the board aren't the best. Those extremely long trade route lines are very hard to see the connections between the colonial areas and Europe. You almost have to put a finger on a trade route and trace it to make sure you aren't "jumping lines" and ending up in the wrong place. I saw several comments on BGG from people making the same complaint. As many stated on BGG, the game is somewhat like combination of Struggle of Empires and Goa. I thought that spot on. I like Endeavor. However its not a good as either of these pseudo-parents.

I saw the designer post he thought it quite normal/proper for many of the colonial area to not open up in a three player game. My view is history-wise this is ridiculous. I mean, could you imagine Spain, France and England deciding, "You know what, phooey on Africa and the Far East. That's too much trouble. We'll merely sail to the Americans and go no further." Yeah, right.  

03/13/10 Played a four-player game with the rules in the box. As I feared only three of six colonial area opened up. The variant should have been used to remove a chit from each trade route, but the owner didn't want to. 
08/28/10 Played a five-player game. As I expected, the game only truly shines with a full compliment. All six colonial areas were opened up.  
04/02/11 Played a three-player game with the variant of removing two chits from each trade route. This worked quite well. Five of the six colonial areas were open by game end. 
03/24/12 Ditto. 01/19/13 Ditto. 05/11/13 Ditto. 07/29/14 a four-player game. 09/12/15 a three-player game.
