RATING: 8
Played twice; it's definitely one to play more than once, as I learned a lot during the first game. 

This is, to some extent, an area control game at its heart: players build up their ability to place workers on the board to control areas.  When placing workers on the board players get benefits, and must plan their moves to choose between being able to build better buildings, getting more workers, being able to use their workers more efficiently, or being able to hold more special power cards.  The buildings players can buy allow players to carry out more actions, but there aren't so many as to lead to confusion.  

One big problem we ran into in our first game is that the cannon building appears to be very strong.  In short, it allows a player to kill an opposing player's worker and replace it with one of their own.  There is no defense against this.  Unfortunately, this allows the attacking player to both get one-time benefits from building a bigger network and also gets them an advantage in controlling the area (since obtaining special-power cards requires a minimum number of workers in an area).  So, if one player is already ahead in an area (say, 3 to 2), then by doing one attack they are now at a 4 to 1 advantage, making it functionally impossible for the losing player to ever gain control of that area (especially since the benefits of building a network are one-time benefits, so even if the losing player attacks they can't get as many rewards).   

However, there are ways to gain influence that are immune from the cannon (by "shipping"), and thus if a player doesn't want to fight it out for influence they don't necessarily have to.  

After the first game I was concerned that the cannon mechanic will make the game frustrating (as, if used multiple times on a player it has the power to knock down players to a point where they can't recover), but I think it is okay, as long as players understand how it works and plan accordingly (either get cannons to fight it out, or avoid placing workers in cannon-prone areas.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This and El Grande are the perfect 5p games. Light enough on rules that anyone can play, and yet really tactical.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It's remarkably odd that Endeavor and Hansa Teutonica came out in the same year, as they're basically the same game only sliced and diced in different directions. Hansa is combative, but mostly non-interactive. Endeavor has a really cool collusion mechanism in the shipping lanes, and a really cool timing mechanism reminiscent of the type of thing you find in a great worker placement game where you have to prioritize your action choices to prevent getting blocked by the other players or leaving anything too enticing open for them or even opening up new possibilities for them.

Hansa focuses on getting the various game actions ["getting more control markers", "deploying / redeploying control markers", "gaining better / more actions", "gaining bonus abilities", etc] into the right phasing and most of the player interaction seems to be about getting out of phase with the other players, while most of the strategy seems to be about optimizing your own action timing. Endeavor puts all of this into a very rigid order [while maintaining nearly all of the same action types as Hansa], making player interaction and strategy about what you do with the actions.

I've had people recommend me Hansa Teutonica time and time again when I ask for a game with a "broad" decision tree where you have a lot of options available to you on any given turn [something like Tigris or Antiquity], but I haven't felt compelled by the game at all the two or three times I've played. It strikes me as a very straight-forward Euro optimization / action timing game like Puerto Rico, Goa, Notre Dame, etc. There's just nothing interesting here to me. Endeavor is the game all of these well-meaning people should have been recommending to me all this time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In the new world, there are 7 rounds in this game. Each round, you construct a building. Next you collect colonists. Then you put colonists into buildings to take actions: (ship to Europe or one of the outlying regions, draw cards, attack another player). Then you put back colonists from occupied buildings into your supply, and start a new round. Each player has a player mat to track their abilities and the cards they collect.

This is a good game. Your actions are fairly simple, and there are many tough choices. There are some instances where it is possible to lose your token in your area when you are attacked, but it is expensive to attack. This game really grows on you, and forces you to make tough choices from turn to turn.

RATING: 6
Endeavor is a good game, but it puts up too many 'Do Not Purchase' flags to warrant ownership.

Primarily, the amount of choices available from outset are so vast and yet the number of actions per turn are so limited that, early on, novices are highly likely to make decisions that will negatively affect their progress for the rest of the game. Even the limited options for 1st turn building choices will massively affect future turns.

Fluctuations in player's tracks in later turns due to the gaining and discarding of cards can be difficult for some to keep track of correctly.

Later turns also are very limited in choices, making for analysis paralysis as players try to calculate the optimal course.

Finally, endgame scoring takes forever.

That said, I'd sit down to a game of Endeavor whenever, but in no way would I consider owning it.

RATING: 9
Played once with SoloPlay's 2 player variant and 3 times with SoloPlay solitaire rules.

I really liked the concept behind this game based on reviews and feedback given.  It reduces the whole explore and exploit concept down to the combination of a few actions.

I think I like it so much because I get such a feeling of accomplishment through a really smooth economic engine.  Somehow the seeming disparate acts (Shipping, Occupying, Drawing/maneuvering politically for gain perhaps?) generates an increasingly powerful engine that allows me to do more.

I like that the randomness is in the setup.  And wow, is it ever random!  After that, it is your choices that matter.

I like fiddling with bits that matter.  All of those chips can and will make a difference.

Why this is not a 10:
-> I like variety

RATING: 5
"Mostly abstract action-point area-control game. I don't like area control, plus this one has some direct pvp attacks."

What I like:
- Finished in 90 minutes with 4p, 2 first-timers
- The basic flow of play is simple and clean
- There appear to be multiple paths to victory

What I don't like:
- Players are STRONGLY encouraged to take a slavery card
- It is way too hard to abolish slavery in a 4p game (and presumably 5p)
- The slavery card symbol is too hard to see from across the table
- Some of the rules are complex and hard to remember
- There are some direct attacks

It's a decent game, but no way is it worth $70 or more.

I think the BGG-voted 2.9 weight is a reasonable assessment, partly because it "feels" like a serious game.

RATING: 6
Evitai di provare Endeavour/Magister Navis a Essen perché a un primo impatto rimasi dubbioso: da una parte l'ottima grafica a tema dei componenti (edifici, plance, carte), dall'altra il tabellone di gioco poco convincente per i miei gusti. 
Magister Navis è un gioco con buonissime idee (gli indicatori della nazione che determinano il livello di costruzione, popolazione, economia e politica), meccaniche tradizionali ben implementate (le maggioranze nelle colonie che indicano la qualità di carte acquistabili) e qualche finezza (la carta che abolisce la schiavitù mettendo in crisi chi fino a quel momento l'ha sfruttata). Poi il gioco scorre liscio come l'olio e ha una durata contenuta. Il gioco però conferma le mie impressioni in terra tedesca. Tutta quell'ambientazione, ben resa e molto presente nelle plance di ogni giocatore, viene meno nel tabellone: poco più di un grafo molto, troppo astratto per quello che dovrebbe rappresentare. Il criterio stesso di assegnazione casuale dei segnalini nelle città neutrali e nei collegamenti (senza nessuna specificità geografica) non aiuta a togliersi dalla bocca quel sapore di astratto che potevi "vestire" senza problemi con un'ambientazione spaziale, medievale o contemporanea. 
I pregi sono comunque superiori ai difetti e il gioco è tutto sommato molto carino.

I could say Endeavor felt a bit like a mix between Struggle of Empires, AoEIII, Puerto Rico and ...um... Thurn & Taxis. But that would probably not say much anyway - thing is, Endeavor plays very unique.

More abstract than I anticipated, more abstract than SoE of course, but Endeavor also had less richness in theme and feel than both AoEIII and Puerto Rico as well. There was almost a sense of remoteness to the setting.

A couple of strategies that can be combined in a multitude of ways, an "easy to overview but hard to predict" kind of game. I had fun playing this one and I bet it has strong replayability.

One play so far - rating may change

RATING: 5.5
First, when you get down to it, Endeavor provides nothing new. That's not necessarily a bad thing, and that doesn't make it a bad game, but it does mean I can get my fix in other games I think are better.

Second, the game feels overly balanced. I've tried multiple strategies, but they really aren't so different. I found that all strategies led to the same end. Increase A to facilitate the increase of B/C/D to facilitate the increase of A/C/D, etc... A high level of specialization is a losing strategy because you need some of everything. The only difference is where you start, thus repeated plays started to feel the same regardless of how I proceeded to increase my tracks. Highly tactical.

Third, in my experience the game is won and lost in the cards. It's probably not all that surprising, but you can't win without taking higher numbered cards. However, the cards just aren't that interesting. Higher levels provide moderate increases over lower levels, but acquiring the cards you want/need can be tedious.

Ultimately, to me it's a pretty dull exercise in efficiency and min/maxing. The game likes you to think you have all kinds of strategic choices, but all you've really got is a couple hours of tactical reactions to the current state of the game when it gets back to your turn. Additionally, while the game plays better with more players, it also increases the play time to such a degree that it isn't worth the time investment, but playing with three players doesn't provide enough tension to make the game worthwhile.

Having said all that, I enjoyed my plays, but it has run its course.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Still loving this fantastic game of balancing your tableau improvements and board presence with lots of interaction. Across 7 rounds, you choose a new building and add it to your player board. Then your player board's stats in various categories determine how many disks you get. When a building's action allows you to place a disc on a space, you collect the token that was randomly assigned to that space at the start of the game. Between the spaces are routes, and when you claim both sides of the route, you also claim the token between them. New areas open up as ship disks come to the board, and powerful cards become available for players who invested discs into those regions. The ultimate goal is to hold spots on the board, and increase the stats on your player board to their highest scoring threshold through collecting those tokens or cards.

It's a fantastic game of balancing your tableau building with your board presence and lots of player interaction as players vie to be the one to finish a route, or compete over tokens or knock other players out of locations. The game demands 4 or 5 players to shine, because it doesn't have any scaling for fewer player counts, but it's one of those really elegant and satisfying mid weight euros .

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Surprisingly quick for a game with lots of different options.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I was slightly underwhelmed by Endeavor on my first play, but afterwards I realised that was because I'd been expecting something more thematic and involved. Reading some of the interesting articles by the designers made me realise how well they'd achieved their goal of a slick, fast-playing development game. It's impressively easy to explain to newcomers, can comfortably play in 20 mins per player and offers lots of choices, both strategic and tactical. My second play was really enjoyable as I could actually decide on a plan and optimise it. My only slight criticisms revolve around its almost abstract nature. There's nothing to really distinguish the different areas you are colonising (not even names for the cities) and so you're just hunting for the symbols that match your strategy. The only exception is the abolition of slavery mechanic; it might have been nice to have a few more of these 'bells and whistles', but I can also see why the designers sacrificed them in the interests of elegance. Other minor niggles are the fiddly set-up and the potential for AP when drawing and discarding in the final round.

RATING: 7.12
Endeavour operates by placing workers, which in turn enhances your score-track in one of four different 'progress' ratings. These then supply more workers or resources. And so on.
It escalates quite nicely, each player getting more and more actions with each passing round (there are seven in total). There is a lot less player interaction than in other games of this general type (Age of Empires/Caylus/Keymarket/Le Havre) but over-all I think the game is all the better for it. It feels tight and streamlined, with simple easy to learn but genuinely difficult to master mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In Endeavor you are trying to win glory for your empire through occupying cities, opening trade routes, becoming governor of a new region, and expanding the wealth, culture, and political prowess of your empire.  The game lasts 7 rounds, in which players, in turn, each build one building, then gain new workers, then pay the salary of last turn's workers, then take actions (governed by the buildings they have built).  Players must balance their ability to build, their ability to recruit new workers, their ability to pay for the workers they have, with their ability to open shipping routes and occupy cities.

At the end of the game cities are worth points, advances in building, culture, money, political influence, some cards, and any recruited but idle workers are worth points.  Players can also gain an extra point for each pair of adjacent cities.  The tension in the game is bound up in managing limited resources within only 7 turns.  Players are also capturing cities or opening trade routes vying for the better position on the board.  Players can interact by using an attack action, which is costly but can gain that coveted city.

The game has a good depth and breadth of strategy.  The board layout is randomized each game play, so the replay value is high; no two games will employ the same tactics, and may require different strategies.

RATING: 8
Seems like there are multiple ways to play, and with just the right amount of aggression for my taste - it has purpose because it's usually used to secure a connection. It's kind of light and forgiving, with the need to plan ahead a little bit. Unfortunately it's also very, very fiddly and has had a surgical theme extraction for the sake of Germanising the game. It's all stuff we've seen before, but this game turns it all into something I'll be happy to explore quite a bit further.

The spelling irks me. I'll be selling my USA copy and replacing it with the German one - a nicer box size to boot.

It would also be really nice if future editions had the city names on the board. It would allow me be more descriptive during the game rather than just saying "this city" and "that city" while pointing.

RATING: 7
I think of the game as supporting two main play styles, and combinations between the two. There is macro, which is mostly about taking culture and finance at every opportunity, and there is teching, which is all about getting and using more efficient buildings. You can go to one extreme or take a more balanced approach. 

The way attacks work, if someone gets attacked repeatedly throughout the game, it is very difficult for them to win. Most of the points come from the tracks, but you can get some from cards, some from buildings, and some from the board. Having some board presence is important, because colonize is so points efficient. 

There is a good amount of strategy, you can pursue different paths throughout the game, and it plays fast. When I play with experienced players, games usually take about 45 minutes.

RATING: 10
I helped playtest Endeavor, and loved it every time. One major change suggested by Z-Man after they accepted it from Carl&Jarrat moved it from being "great" to "awesome" - they put tokens & glory points in between the cities to be earned only if you got the cities on both sides - adding another layer of interaction and making the military option a lot more effective.  

Here are the key things I like about this game: 

It's short! Can even take less than an hour if everyone knows what they're doing, but even if they don't it should never take more than 2 hours. 

Given how short it is, it handles a *lot* of aspects of its theme in an elegant and abstract sort of way: Expansion, war, shipping, trade, colonization, slavery, culture, finance, politics - who would of thought you could fit all of that into a light/medium weight 60-90 minute game? 

It's "luckless" - after the initial random setup of the board chits, which are visible to everyone, there's no luck involved at all, except the choices of your fellow players, which you can influence...

There's no real downtime or analysis paralysis - every turn requires a fair bit of thought, but there's no point in thinking things too far ahead because what the other players do is always going to affect what you can or should do next. 

Oh, and now I've finally played on a real board rather than a paper prototype, I can confirm the artwork and component quality are absolutely top notch!

RATING: 8
This is just a solid design.  All the parts work together and this is one fine tuned machine - it's one fast and lean Euro.  Endeavor is more forgiving than Princes of Florence but it has that same angst created by a fixed number of turns.  The gaming payoff here is setting up a powerful turn 6 and 7.  I also love the fact there is direct conflict for control of the board - something a lot of Euros are afraid to tackle - but it's done right here - not random, nasty and worthwhile.  In my opinion right now given the length of play time, the ease of teaching the game, and the multiple strategies - this THE medium-light weight Euro to have in your collection.

RATING: 7
This is a reasonably uncomplicated game where almost all the decisions seem to be based on opportunity costs and that makes it very, very interesting.

The majority of the game involves taking control of areas of a map in order to collect symbols that improve your abilities as well as unlock new areas and draw cards, also with symbols on them.  The reason so much of the game involves opportunity costs is because every time you take an action you open up new possibilities for other players.  When you take a spot on a shipping lane you make a new symbol available to your opponents and you also progress towards a card draw for the player with majority.  When you take a colony opponents need to decide whether to take the corresponding symbol to prevent you from collecting an additional symbol and also whether they even want to have the corresponding symbol and whether the additional symbol even helps you much.  There's also a lot more.

This is a neat game because it is so accessible and considering all this stuff makes for a deep and rich experience.  There's always a balance to consider between progressing the game and what you are giving up to others by progressing it because you always have the opportunity to stall but this can create stand-offs.  Very well designed and interesting system.

RATING: N/A
I was kind of disappointed on this one. It's a fair game with some difficult choices but it doesn't add anything new. It's a classic eurogame, short in gameplay and excellent components. I won't mind playing it occasionally because it has no mistakes. It's a solid design. I guess it doesn't work that well with 3 players only.

One of the things I like on this game it's the fact that it has several paths to victory. In that sense it's not a dry mechanical game. Players can find different strategies and aiming VP's in a different manner.

I don't like the fact that players need other players help to make discoveries because it always seem unfair to a third party if someone helps a player getting an extra action token.
Military actions seem quiet relevant to gameplay and I really think that it is the most important aspect of the game because it determines a few but major tweaks to final scoring. Military actions are not that expensive and are not avoidable thus being fundamental to the game result.

Setup is dull.

RATING: 7
I played a demo of this at Gencon a couple years ago and it wasn't described as well as it could have been(or its possible that I was sleep deprived) and I missed the attack options.  Imagine my suprise where in this seemingly dry euro, someone starts blowing my shit up.  I was immediately soured and it quickly dropped off my radar.  But then soemone brought it out at a recent game night and I knew that I needed to give it a second shot.  Well, I am glad I did.  It is a decent worker placement, with a little bit of competition and a smattering of conflict. 

The random chit placement adds a little replayability, but really I don't think your strategy is tied that closely to the token placments.  Sure, the token placemnet will alter your choices on where to place your workers; but overall if you (for example) like a shipping strategy, you are not going to have to abondon that strategy based on the token distribution.  

My opinion went from meh, to I'd play it again. I increased my rating for a 5 to a 7.

RATING: 8.5
just one, 3-player game so far, but looks extremely promising.  The rules and actions are very simple and straightforward, and the the only luck is the chit randomization at the start of the game. This looks to place the game in one of my favored categories--games where you define your strategy at the very beginning of the game and the clash comes where those strategies collide.

Good stuff and highly likely to go up in score as we play more!

RATING: 8.5
:thumbsup: LIKE
+ Not everybody agrees, but I feel the theme works
+ The turns start out short and get progressively longer as the game advance for a nice learning curve
+ Relatively short play time for the type of game even with 5 players
+ Highly tactical you have to think to repercussion of your move and how to best optimize your empire
+ The randomization of the token means good replay value

:thumbsdown: DISLIKE
- Pretty boring with 3 players as most region don't open up.
- It's pretty fiddly with all the cards, discs, cubes and tokens. Also needs lots of table space with the player boards.
- The end game needs some work.  If you're the player in the lead be prepare to be bombarded

:star: VERDICT
For the playing time and the replay value involve, this is a great game for 4-5 players.  I like the tactical nature of the game, but hate the cannon fest that ends the game.

RATING: 6
I'm pretty sure the game's name comes from the fact that it is another fairly dry cube-pusher that's "endeavoring" to pass itself off as a thematic game.  It's not a bad game, though.  It's a delightfully simple game to learn, and the various game elements do make thematic sense in their own abstract way.  With enough players, there's a decent amount of player interaction in the form of competition for various things and the occasional bit of rather costly direct confrontation.  However, the experienced players in our 5-player game were united in their belief that the game ONLY plays well with 5 players, no fewer.  My own opinion based on a single play was that the game was decent enough and I would play it again if asked, but it wasn't interesting enough for me to request another play.  Game design: 3.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 2.5/5  Total: 6/10

RATING: 5.5
A good, solid euro that I'll happily play but can't imagine ever [i]choosing[/i].

There's an interesting balance of choices in the early game but I don't like the way shipping becomes useless. What I really don't like is what happens with combat in the final turns -- it seems that often attacks by players who are not competitive can play kingmaker among those that are.

Edit: A few years on and I do not feel it has been improved by time. A number of friends rave about this one and I don't understand the appeal. There's not that much going on, there are often elements of inadvertent kingmaking, and I think there is an uncompensated turn order advantage early in the game. Ratings history: 7 -> 6 -> 5.5

RATING: 8
Single play, treated as a learning game, with many fundamental implications & repercussions only becoming apparent as the game progressed (in particular, the rule requiring you to have a certain amount of presence in a region before drawing cards wasn’t factored in until it was too late). Simple & accessible rules disguise a pretty meaty development game. Echoes of older games (Goa, Taj Mahal, Puerto Rico) but Endeavor also feels different. All the bits were a little larger than I’d expected - in a nice way. Keen to try again soon.

Second play: quite different. Two players with one game under their belt, two newbies. Less exploration, more attacking. I was always behind on buildings & population, so limited actions. I scored only 2 points from city occupation.

RATING: 8

RATING: 8
Area control exercise with a colonial theme and no luck. Players have to increase their power to build, populate, pay and politicise, and a balance has to be struck as this needs to be done relatively evenly. Buildings allow players to perform certain actions such as shipping to particular areas of the board, settling territories or conducting military attacks to displace an opponent from a territory. Victory points are awarded for how far along the various status tracks a player has progressed and for how many areas they have settled. There is a slightly abstract aspect to the game but it incorporates its theme very well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played 1+ times.  Neat no-luck game of exploration.  The individual player boards are a little Goa/Industrial waste like in that you are improving  your position in a number of categories and each category gives a different ability.  The board play isn't that similar to a game I've played.  You place workers to control forts or help open a new area of forts.  Each fort is worth 1 point at the end of the game.  Every worker you place also gives you a token good for an advance in one of your player board categories.  
At the end of the game you get points for the level you've reached in each category, your forts and for connections to adjacent forts, and some bonus points for buildings constructed and cards drawn. **UPDATE** plays very quickly which is a plus. 2010/04/08

RATING: 8.5
First play tonight.  Originally, I browsed through the rules and didn't quite "get" it, so put it aside.  This is months later and by luck (we flipped coins) it beat out Cyclades and Dungeon Lords and hit the table.

Man, am I glad it did!!  This game was very fun and both players (3-player game) enjoyed it greatly as well. I suspect a 5-player game might have more gunfire (ie cannon use) than we had.

Overall, an awesome game!

A fejlődési sávok miatt a Goára hasonlít. Nincs benne aukciós rész, cserébe van egy többségi mechanizmus a kormányzó (Governor) kártyák megszerzésénél. RATING: 8
This has some elements of Puerto Rico (occupying buildings), Taj Mahal (picking up bonus tiles while placing influence on the board and making connections) and Goa (chits that give you extra actions and increase your abilities on your own development board/tracks). While it has borrowed mechanics from a lot of sources, it has its own unique feel. Endeavor also has an awful lot going on. Players have tons of options, many possible paths to victory to pursue and difficult decisions to make (especially when building - the most important choice a player will make each round). There's always more you want to do than resources allow, so each action is crucial. There's plenty of depth here, but experienced players should be able to knock back a four-player game in under an hour, which is a nice bang for the time invested. My one small quibble is the attack action creates a get-the-leader dynamic in the game, which can really hose a player (especially if other players incorrectly assess who is actually the leader). Endeavor also is a game, like Puerto Rico, where inexperienced players can give the game away to another player by "setting them up" for a big turn too often. With players of roughly the same experience level, however, Endeavor is a tense, challenging game that plays quickly despite the depth it offers.

RATING: 7
Played twice with 4 players. Played one rule wrong (did not notice that one could ship to a region with a full shipping track), but that didn't affect the game too much. The first game was a little rough as most of the players didn't realize the importance of shipping, so quickly fell behind and it was tough to recover. That first game didn't have much colonization as many provinces weren't even close to having full shipping lanes. The second game was completely different as all shipping lanes filled up and the board was 85% filled. Doing well at this game is a fine-balancing act as one must improve all four status tracks to advance. Beautiful components, a fully functional game system, and a good bang-for-the-buck game. Looking forward to trying this with 5 as the confrontation should get even cutthroat. The only downside to this game is that there is no way to defend against an attack; one must prepare themselves to be attacked and not grow too attached to one's occupations.

RATING: 3
I've seen someone mention it before and unfortunately it turned out just like it: this game allows for situations in which you are taken out of the game early on and can't do anything about it. For the rest of the game you will then be able to exist along the other players without any chance of really getting your economic engine started. 

The gamebreaker: While attacking someone is painful for the attacker, it is also very lucrative and it's backbreakingly painful for the one who is being attacked, especially early on in the game. It's even more annoying as the attacker chooses arbitrarily in the beginning who to attack. You cannot see at that point  who's good in the game or not. And you can do nothing to prevent it. 

I see some nice ideas in the game, which is probably why this game receives so many BGG praises. However I am astonished by this games's rating overall. One of my coplayers witnessed the very same problems in his one session he played the game before. And the problem is not solved by a learning curve. You can be experienced all you want. If two people choose to attack you in round 2, that's it.  

Maybe the game was so cutthroat because we were 5 players. The only way I see me giving this game one more chance is with 3 players. Cause knowing in round 2 that there will be no way of coming back into the game however hard you try is just sitting the game out for the remaining 1 1/2 hours. The finals scores showed that I just happened instead of playing. 

54-52-45-42-32

And also I fail to see what the many different strategies are supposed to be for this game. I'd make a bet if you don't settle in Europe at the start of the game and instead try to aim for a long-term strategy it won't work. 

Highly overrated, overhyped.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Much has already been written about this first published design of Carl de Visser and Jarratt Gray, and whatever else anyone thinks of this game, it would be hard to argue with the fact that this is a very impressive first published design for any designer. It's a fairly complex German-style board game with a number of interlocking systems that take elements from many popular earlier designs (such as Puerto Rico and Goa) and repurposes them into a new package that works. The mechanics all work very well together which in and of itself is very impressive. It's also not a game that I can really adequately judge after just two plays and I need to play a few more times, but after those two games I'm not sure it's a game for me. My issue with the game, which I previously discussed in my Essen 2009: GeekBuzz Meets Fairplay article, is that it is yet another in a long line of games that give players the opportunity to specialize, but discourage and effectively prevent such specialization by rewarding a generalist approach that does a bit of everything. Like Agricola, Endeavor is a game that allows you to score points in a variety of categories and determines the ultimate result by adding up your points in each of these possible categories. However, you can't really specialize and pick a unique path to follow as you attempt to get as many points as possible because the way in which the technology advancement works seems to prevent such specialization. I tend to prefer games where I can actually take the game up on its offer of specialization (such as Age of Empires 3 and Container), rather than being corralled into the same generic approach as everyone else.

RATING: 6.5
Played for years that you couldn't see what you were getting when you took a token from the board. Rated it 6 for being asinine. Replaying it with correct rules (so it is luckless) it's a bit better.

Basically this is a bottleneck/efficiency euro where drafting an improvement (to your economy) involves placing a disc of your color on a specific region on the map. Some regions/spaces need to get drafted before others (so sometimes you don't want to take something because a later player will get something great), and some pairs of spaces are connected so that the first person controlling both gets an extra improvement.

Moves are small in effect. You want to prioritize and time the improvements you take so that you'll have leveled something up when it next pays out.

The game is kind of a snowball, where you spend more than half of the turns doing very little, and then in the last couple of turns do quite a bit more, and then the game is over. A little strange.

I still don't like the scoring much. Scores are not quick to count up and are usually very close. There are a couple of things where I don't understand why you get points for them (labor you were unable to use, and a bonus for sucking so much that you didn't have a Governor card to store on the free space you get for one).

On the whole, a pretty painless game that would probably be irritating if it were much longer. I do like the idea of tying the drafting of economic improvements to spaces on a map. But I wish the map were more important.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
A game of world exploration and empire building. You represent a European empire colonizing the Mediterranean and shipping to all parts of the world to increase the empire's glory and status in Industry, Culture, Finance and Politics. When a region opens up (i.e. the shipping lanes are full) a player can colonize, attack or retrieve resources from that region. The game plays easily as each turn a player builds a building giving him a special action or increasing one of his main statistics, and gets a number of people with which to activate buildings for special actions (Ship, Occupy, Attack, Draw or Pay). Increased skill in Politics allow the player to hold cards that help increase his statistics and glory.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5

So, it happens too often that someone takes an action for the small effect that ends up seriously hurting you (since you were counting on taking it for the large effect) entirely by accident. Damaging other players' plans on purpose is one thing, but who wants to play a game of accidental screw jobs?

080311: Played again with four. A much better experience than with 5, but still not terribly impressed with the game. RATING: 7
Excellent euro game, in the vein of Puerto Rico but different enough, more variability in setup but still a no-randomness game which I like a lot.

[+] perfect information game, fast paced, rather short game (only 7 turns), multiple paths to victory, multiple sources of interactions: race for governor, area/route control, a bit of fighting, many things to manage/optimize.
[-] no adjustments depending of number of players, might run into small issues in 3p/5p. 4p seems to be perfect.

want to play more, rating might go up.

RATING: 8
(9/09) After one 4p game - It is an enjoyable Euro game that might have gotten a little extra hype from GenCon.  Solid game play, interesting choices - what track do I research/build on, where should I ship, do I do a card draw, what is my hand size, can I be governor, extra points for "links", will slavery be abolished?

There are a lot of ways to score and the game is new enough that the subtle ways to gain an advantage are not super obvious so you can explore a different strategy.  There is the potential for AP - so maybe a "turn clock" is in order.  I can see future games getting done in 90 minutes with people who have played before and make decisions rather quickly.

Want to play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Resource collection and some drafting (buildings, cards). Minor direct interaction via wars (that can occasionally be annoying and lead to kingmaking), but mostly it is area majority.

Very fast first turns (when you have just an action or two), while later ones can be quite longer (and with more options, since areas of the boards get "unlocked" during the game). Overall, the length feels right and the decisions not trivial.

Deterministic gameplay, but random setup to offer greater replay value.

Theme is inexistent.

Nice game in its genre (optimization of actions, building an economic engine, scoring VPs to win), albeit it lacks a "soul" (i.e. some good reason to play it rather than the other hundreds of similar - and in some cases better - games).

RATING: 8
To be so much more of all the same things you've seen in a Euro before (colonizing, shipping, wooden discs, VPs, and whatnot), this game really manages to distill out all of the sameness and make itself into something much more than the sum of its parts.  It's hard to really explain what makes the game so appealing, but it's turns are simple to understand, intuitive to play, and the interaction is high.  In addition, it's a perfect information game, so your decisions are meaningful, and has a lot of replay value due to its random setup each game.  I don't think it's for everyone (if you're not going to start battling each other for possession of territories, then the game won't reach its potential), but for me, it's very enjoyable.

4 plays: 4 players
6 plays: 5 players

RATING: 7
This game seems like it has a lot more going on and should take much longer to play but it's a nice little Euro package. I like how the decks are set up and the game has a built-in timer in the building phase. I'm not crazy about the random token setup as it's tough to get certain tracks going that you need early if the tokens aren't on the cities when you can only occupy. I guess you can specialize more by drawing from the decks since certain areas are better at certain tracks. The buildings are a bit generic too.

The 2-player variant works pretty well but I have a feeling this game is really for 4-5. I'm hoping 3-player is a little better with an actual brain and strategy controlling the third player. The problem I had is that the neutral player ended up screwing me more than it helped me or blocked my opponent. What I thought was a clever play would open up a port or block my own connections. Drawing seemed to be the worst, since most of the time there were only a few open ports with neutral presence so I'd be forced to discard a 2 and give the opponent access to the better 3.

Sold. Tough to get 4-5 for this and the 2-player wasn't really what I hoped for.

RATING: 10
This game packs a lot of strategy in a short amount of time.  It is a game where building a balanced empire is critical.  Sure you can invest in a heavy industry, but with out finance and population to use the high end buildings your empire is doomed.

Have played the 2p variant and with multiple players.  It plays quickly - I played two 3p games with rules explanation, set-up and clean-up in under two hours.

The game is playable with two, but much more fun with more.  I think the game is optimum with four and a 2p variant where each player controls two colors would be an excellent variant. Of course the Knizia rule (highest low score wins) would need to be in effect.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Endeavor features an extremely streamlined gameplay that makes each round fairly quick to resolve.  The area majority aspect of the shipping track as well as the ability to attack add a small dose of interaction to an otherwise solitaire affair.  My only concern for the game itself is the technology tracks.  Since all four are evaluated at the same time for every players, one is almost expected to develop each equally with very little variation.  Which to me kind of goes against what a technology tree is for.  It should allow different paths to victory depending on my choices, but not so in Endeavor.  It's apparent that if you want to do well, you can't just devote your resources to one or two tracks.  As a result the player boards will look very similar at the end for those with scores that are close to each other.

Furthermore, even though the trade tokens are randomized, the buildings and the asset cards remain the same.  Therefore, I imagine the replayability will suffer.  There are just not enough different strategies to choose from.

I think Endeavor is a game that gives really good first impression, but will eventually fail to please as you get more familiar with it.  I'm going to have to try it again to see if it's the case.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
And of course misscored on the first play... note to self:
Player Board:
Industry Track (0-15 points)
Culture Track (0-15 points)
Finance Track (0-15 points)
Politics Track (0-15 points)
University (3 points per university)
Resource Cards (various points)
"Free Governor" space empty (3 points)
Population tokens remaining in your harbor (1 point per 3Pop)
Discarded Slavery Cards (-1 per card)

Main Board:
Cities (1 or 2 points per city)
Connections (1 point per connection)
